| `[Foundational] Morphology of narrative` `[Formalist]`          | *Morphology of the Folktale*, Russian original 1928; English trans. 1958; revised 1968 `[Foundational]` `[Evergreen]` | **Vladimir Propp**; trans. **Laurence Scott**         | Propp analyses Russian wonder tales by recurrent narrative functions rather than by theme, character psychology, or historical origin. His model shows how seemingly diverse tales can share a deep sequential grammar of actions. Its power lies in formal abstraction; its limitation is corpus dependence, because the model works best for a restricted class of folktales and cannot simply be generalized to all narrative. It remains indispensable for understanding narrative grammar, sequence, and structural analysis.                                                                                                                  | Suitable for readers interested in form, folklore, genre, and early structuralism. No advanced theory required, but later comparison with Lévi-Strauss, Greimas, and Barthes is useful. |
| `[Foundational] Structuralist poetics` `[Structuralist]`        | *The Poetics of Prose*, French original 1971; English trans. 1977 `[Intermediate]` `[Foundational]`                   | **Tzvetan Todorov**; trans. **Richard Howard**        | Todorov helped consolidate narratology as a structural poetics concerned with grammar, genre, transformation, and narrative order. The book is historically important because it shows the moment when narrative study wanted to become a systematic science of forms. Its limitation is that its structuralist ambition can seem too abstract for historically situated reading. It is best used to understand the theoretical climate from which Genette and later narratology emerged.                                                                                                                                                           | Suitable after basic structuralism or formalism. Best read alongside Barthes and Genette. Less useful as a practical manual than Bal or Abbott.                                         |
| `[Foundational] Structuralist narratology` `[Structuralist]`    | *Narrative Discourse: An Essay in Method*, French original 1972; English trans. 1980 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]`       | **Gérard Genette**; trans. **Jane E. Lewin**          | Genette supplies the classical vocabulary for narrative time, mood, voice, order, duration, frequency, focalization, narrative level, and metalepsis. Its influence is enormous because it made narratology into a precise analytic language. Its limitation is also its strength: it is highly taxonomic and heavily anchored in Proust, so beginners can mistake its categories for universal laws. Terminology note: French `récit`, `histoire`, and `narration` do not map perfectly onto English “discourse,” “story,” and “narrating.”                                                                                                        | Essential for serious narratology. Best read after Bal or Abbott. Advanced readers should pair it with later debates on focalization, rhetoric, and transmediality.                     |
| `[Foundational] Story and discourse` `[Structuralist]` `[Film]` | *Story and Discourse: Narrative Structure in Fiction and Film*, 1978 `[Intermediate]` `[Evergreen]`                   | **Seymour Chatman**                                   | Chatman offers one of the clearest structuralist accounts of narrative as the relation between story and discourse, and he extends the model to both fiction and film. The book is valuable for connecting literary narratology with medium questions before transmedial narratology became central. Its limitation is that the communication model can feel too neat, especially for experimental fiction, unreliable narration, and interactive media. It remains a strong bridge between classical narratology and media narratology.                                                                                                            | Suitable after Abbott or Bal. Especially useful for readers working on fiction-film comparison. Should be supplemented with film-specific narrative theory.                             |

### 1.3 Narration, Voice, Focalization, Perspective & Reliability

| Type & Subdomain                                                                        | Title, Edition, Year                                                                                                                                       | Author / Editor / Translator                   | Description & Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                 | Target Audience & Characteristics                                                                                                                                         |
| --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| `[Foundational] Rhetorical fiction theory` `[Rhetorical]`                               | *The Rhetoric of Fiction*, 2nd ed., 1983; first ed. 1961 `[Foundational]` `[Evergreen]`                                                                    | **Wayne C. Booth**                             | Booth’s central contribution is to treat fiction as rhetorical communication among implied author, narrator, text, and reader. The book gives the classic account of unreliable narration and remains unavoidable for debates over implied author and ethical judgment. Its weakness is that Booth sometimes moralizes narrative form and treats authorial communication more confidently than later poststructuralist or cognitive approaches would allow. The second edition includes Booth’s afterword, where he clarifies misunderstandings and revises some earlier positions.  | Essential for rhetorical narratology. Suitable after a basic narratology introduction. Best paired with Phelan, Rabinowitz, and later critiques of implied author theory. |
| `[Research] Narratee and narrative communication` `[Structuralist]` `[Rhetorical]`      | *Narratology: The Form and Functioning of Narrative*, 1982 `[Intermediate]` `[Reference]`                                                                  | **Gerald Prince**                              | Prince gives a precise account of narrative communication, including narrator, narratee, narrative grammar, events, and levels. The book is useful because it sharpens terms that are often used loosely in literary criticism. Its limitation is that its structuralist model can underplay historical, ideological, affective, and medium-specific differences. It remains valuable for conceptual discipline, especially around narratee and narrative situation.                                                                                                                                                   | Suitable after Bal or Genette. Useful for students who need exact terminology. Less effective as an introduction for readers without literary theory background.          |
| `[Research] Focalization and narrative perspective` `[Structuralist]` `[Postclassical]` | *Focalization in Narrative: Theoretical and Critical Essays*, 2001 `[Advanced]` `[Anthology]`                                                              | **Willie van Peer**, **Seymour Chatman**, eds. | This collection documents the major debates around focalization after Genette. It is useful because “who sees?” and “who speaks?” are often confused in basic literary criticism, and the essays show why the distinction remains difficult. Its limitation is that edited volumes vary in focus and are not linear teaching texts. It is best used after Genette, Bal, and Booth.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                     | Suitable for advanced undergraduates and graduate students. Good for research on point of view, perspective, consciousness representation, and unreliable narration.      |
| `[Research] Unreliable narration` `[Rhetorical]` `[Cognitive]`                          | *Unreliable Narration: Studien zur Theorie und Praxis unglaubwürdigen Erzählens in der englischsprachigen Erzählliteratur*, 1998 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Ansgar Nünning**, ed.                        | Nünning’s work is important because it shifts unreliability from a purely author-centered model toward readerly inference, textual signals, norms, frames, and interpretive contexts. This corrects the tendency to treat unreliability as merely a defect in a narrator’s truthfulness. Its limitation is that the approach can become diffuse if every interpretive instability becomes unreliability. It is valuable for advanced work on narration, ethics, and cognition.                                                                                                                                         | Best after Booth and Phelan. German-language background may help depending on the edition or essay. Useful for research on modernist and postmodern fiction.              |
| `[Research] Metalepsis and narrative levels` `[Structuralist]` `[Postclassical]`        | *Metalepsis: From Figure to Fiction*, French original 2004; English trans. 2015 `[Advanced]` `[Research]`                                                  | **Gérard Genette**; trans. **Patricia Wahl**   | Genette’s later work on metalepsis extends his theory of narrative levels into boundary-crossing between narrator, storyworld, authorial plane, and fictional ontology. It matters because postmodern fiction, film, comics, and games often exploit precisely these crossings. Its limitation is that Genette’s account is still more taxonomic than media-theoretical. It should be read with later transmedial and unnatural narratology.                                                                                                                                                                           | Suitable for advanced readers after *Narrative Discourse*. Best for research on metafiction, frame-breaking, ontological play, and postmodern narrative.                  |

### 1.4 Narrative Time, Plot, Event, Character & Progression

| Type & Subdomain                                                                     | Title, Edition, Year                                                                                                             | Author / Editor / Translator                 | Description & Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                     | Target Audience & Characteristics                                                                                                                                          |
| ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| `[Foundational] Narrative time` `[Structuralist]`                                    | *Narrative Discourse: An Essay in Method*, 1980 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]`                                                       | **Gérard Genette**; trans. **Jane E. Lewin** | Genette’s account of order, duration, and frequency remains the standard point of departure for narrative temporality. It explains analepsis, prolepsis, scene, summary, pause, ellipsis, singulative narration, repetitive narration, and iterative narration. Its limitation is that it analyses time primarily through literary discourse, especially Proust, and needs supplementing for film, games, and oral storytelling. It is still the strongest technical source for classical narrative time.  | Essential for serious work on temporal structure. Best read after a general introduction. Pair with Ricoeur, Richardson, and film narratology for broader temporal theory. |
| `[Research] Plot and progression` `[Rhetorical]`                                     | *Reading People, Reading Plots: Character, Progression, and the Interpretation of Narrative*, 1989 `[Intermediate]` `[Research]` | **James Phelan**                             | Phelan links plot to character, readerly judgment, and rhetorical progression rather than treating it as a neutral sequence of events. The book is valuable because it shows how narrative movement produces interpretive and ethical effects over time. Its limitation is that it is most at home in literary fiction and less directly applicable to games or database narratives. It is a foundational work for rhetorical narratology’s account of progression.                                        | Suitable after Booth and Bal. Useful for readers studying plot, character, audience response, and ethical interpretation.                                                  |
| `[Research] Character theory` `[Structuralist]` `[Cognitive]`                        | *Character and Person*, 1986 `[Advanced]` `[Research]`                                                                           | **Uri Margolin**                             | Margolin’s work is important for treating character not merely as a psychological imitation of persons but as a textual, semantic, and cognitive construct. It helps distinguish character as textual entity, storyworld individual, and readerly model. Its limitation is that the work is theoretically demanding and less convenient than textbook discussions. It is essential for serious research on character ontology and interpretation.                                                          | Best for advanced students. Requires familiarity with possible worlds theory or semantic approaches to fiction. Useful after Bal, Chatman, and cognitive narratology.      |
| `[Research] Narrative sequence and eventfulness` `[Structuralist]` `[Postclassical]` | *Narratology: An Introduction*, 2003 English trans. `[Intermediate]` `[Advanced]`                                                | **Wolf Schmid**                              | Schmid offers a systematic account of narrative constitution, eventfulness, perspective, narrator, and composition, with strong roots in German and Russian narratology. It is especially helpful for event theory because it asks what makes an occurrence narratively significant. Its limitation is that the terminology can feel dense for readers trained only in Anglo-American narrative theory. It adds a valuable non-Anglophone theoretical lineage to the field.                                | Suitable after Bal and Genette. Useful for researchers working on eventfulness, composition, and Slavic/German narratology.                                                |
| `[Research] Possible worlds and fictionality` `[Cognitive]` `[Transmedial]`          | *Possible Worlds, Artificial Intelligence, and Narrative Theory*, 1991 `[Advanced]` `[Research]`                                 | **Marie-Laure Ryan**                         | Ryan imports possible worlds theory and AI-inspired models into narratology to explain immersion, accessibility relations, fictional worlds, and narrative comprehension. The book matters because it shifts narrative theory from surface discourse alone to structured storyworlds and readerly world-construction. Its limitation is that some AI background has aged, but the world-theoretical framework remains influential. It is foundational for later work in transmedial and digital narrative. | Best after basic narratology and some semantics or philosophy of fiction. Useful for narrative worlds, immersion, fictionality, and digital media.                         |

### 1.5 Rhetorical Narratology, Narrative Ethics & Reader Response

| Type & Subdomain                                                       | Title, Edition, Year                                                                                                    | Author / Editor / Translator | Description & Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                     | Target Audience & Characteristics                                                                                                                                   |
| ---------------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| `[Foundational] Rhetorical narratology` `[Rhetorical]`                 | *Reading Narrative as Rhetoric: Technique, Audiences, Ethics, Ideology*, 1996 `[Intermediate]` `[Research]`             | **James Phelan**             | Phelan develops narrative as a purposive communication among authors, narrators, audiences, characters, and values. The book is central because it connects technique with ethics and ideology without dissolving form into politics alone. Its limitation is that the rhetorical model can seem too author-oriented for poststructuralist or strongly reader-response approaches. It is one of the best bridges from classical narrative form to interpretive dynamics.                                   | Suitable after Booth. Strong for students interested in ethics, audience, character, and narrative progression. Best paired with Rabinowitz.                        |
| `[Research] Authorial audience and reading conventions` `[Rhetorical]` | *Before Reading: Narrative Conventions and the Politics of Interpretation*, 1987 `[Intermediate]` `[Evergreen]`         | **Peter J. Rabinowitz**      | Rabinowitz shows that reading begins before textual processing: genre expectations, conventions, authorial audience, and interpretive rules guide what readers notice. This is valuable because it prevents naive claims that meaning simply “emerges” from the text itself. Its limitation is that the model presupposes relatively literate and convention-sensitive readers and is less focused on cognitive universals. It remains fundamental for rhetorical and reception-oriented narratology.      | Suitable after Booth or Phelan. Useful for interpretation, genre, ethics, and teaching narrative analysis.                                                          |
| `[Research] Narrative ethics` `[Ethics]` `[Rhetorical]`                | *The Company We Keep: An Ethics of Fiction*, 1988 `[Intermediate]` `[Research]`                                         | **Wayne C. Booth**           | Booth argues that narratives are ethical companions that shape attention, desire, judgment, and value. Its strength is that it treats reading as a morally consequential practice rather than a purely formal exercise. Its weakness is that its ethical vocabulary can appear normative and humanist in ways later theory contests. It remains important for narrative ethics, especially when read with Phelan and Nussbaum.                                                                             | Suitable for readers interested in ethics, rhetoric, and literary criticism. Best after *The Rhetoric of Fiction*. Not a neutral theory of all narrative reception. |
| `[Research] Narrative affect and ethics` `[Rhetorical]` `[Ethics]`     | *Living to Tell about It: A Rhetoric and Ethics of Character Narration*, 2005 `[Advanced]` `[Research]`                 | **James Phelan**             | Phelan focuses on character narration and the ethical positions of characters, narrators, authors, and audiences. The book is useful because it shows how narration is simultaneously formal, affective, and ethical. Its limitation is that it is best suited to literary texts with strong character narration rather than non-literary or interactive narrative. Cornell presents it as a work on the challenges offered by diverse narratives and character narration.  | Best for advanced readers in rhetorical narratology. Requires Booth and some familiarity with Phelan’s earlier work.                                                |
| `[Bridge] Reader response and interpretation` `[Reception]`            | *The Act of Reading: A Theory of Aesthetic Response*, German original 1976; English trans. 1978 `[Advanced]` `[Bridge]` | **Wolfgang Iser**            | Iser is not narrowly a narratologist, but his theory of gaps, implied reader, anticipation, retrospection, and aesthetic response strongly informs narrative reception. The book helps explain how narratives organize participation rather than merely deliver content. Its limitation is that it belongs to reception theory and phenomenology, so it lacks the technical precision of Genette or Bal. It is valuable when narrative theory meets readerly process.                                      | Suitable after basic narratology and hermeneutics. Useful for reader response, narrative gaps, suspense, and interpretation.                                        |

### 1.6 Cognitive Narratology, Experientiality & Narrative Mind

| Type & Subdomain                                                     | Title, Edition, Year                                                                                              | Author / Editor / Translator                 | Description & Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                    | Target Audience & Characteristics                                                                                                       |
| -------------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| `[Foundational] Natural narratology` `[Cognitive]` `[Postclassical]` | *Towards a “Natural” Narratology*, 1996 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]`                                                | **Monika Fludernik**                         | Fludernik’s central concept is `experientiality`: narrative is grounded not merely in plot or sequence but in the representation and evocation of human experience. This reorients narratology from structural sequence toward cognition, embodiment, consciousness, and natural storytelling frames. Its limitation is that its theoretical density makes it difficult as a first cognitive narratology text. It is one of the decisive works of postclassical narratology.                                                                              | Best after Genette, Bal, and basic cognitive narratology. Essential for research on experientiality, consciousness, and naturalization. |
| `[Research] Cognitive narratology` `[Cognitive]`                     | *Story Logic: Problems and Possibilities of Narrative*, 2002; paperback 2004 `[Advanced]` `[Research]`            | **David Herman**                             | Herman brings narratology into contact with linguistics, cognitive science, mental models, worldmaking, and narrative understanding. The book is valuable because it treats narrative comprehension as a cognitive process rather than only a textual structure. Its limitation is that it is conceptually ambitious and not always easy to operationalize in close reading. The University of Nebraska Press describes the paperback as part of the *Frontiers of Narrative* series and lists it as a 500-page work.  | Best for advanced students. Requires prior narratology and some interest in cognitive science. Pair with Fludernik and Palmer.          |
| `[Research] Fictional minds` `[Cognitive]`                           | *Fictional Minds*, 2004 `[Intermediate]` `[Research]`                                                             | **Alan Palmer**                              | Palmer argues that readers construct fictional minds through social cognition, not merely through direct interior monologue. The book is important because it expands consciousness studies beyond stream of consciousness and free indirect discourse. Its limitation is that it can underplay textual form when emphasizing mind attribution. It is very useful for studying character, social cognition, and collective mental functioning in fiction.                                                                                                 | Suitable after basic character theory and cognitive narratology. Useful for fiction, drama, and narrative psychology.                   |
| `[Research] Consciousness representation` `[Cognitive]` `[Literary]` | *Transparent Minds: Narrative Modes for Presenting Consciousness in Fiction*, 1978 `[Intermediate]` `[Evergreen]` | **Dorrit Cohn**                              | Cohn gives one of the most precise accounts of how fiction represents consciousness: psycho-narration, quoted monologue, narrated monologue, and related forms. Its strength is analytic clarity in a domain often described impressionistically. Its limitation is medium specificity: the model is built for prose fiction and cannot be directly transferred to film or games. It remains indispensable for consciousness in narrative.                                                                                                                | Suitable after basic narratology. Essential for modernist fiction, free indirect discourse, and fictional consciousness.                |
| `[Bridge] Narrative identity` `[Philosophical]` `[Cognitive]`        | *Oneself as Another*, French original 1990; English trans. 1992 `[Advanced]` `[Bridge]`                           | **Paul Ricoeur**; trans. **Kathleen Blamey** | Ricoeur’s account of narrative identity links time, selfhood, action, ethics, and interpretation. It is not a narratology textbook, but it is central for understanding how narrative organizes personal identity and moral self-understanding. Its limitation is philosophical density and indirect relation to technical narratology. It is best treated as a bridge between hermeneutics, ethics, and narrative theory.                                                                                                                                | Suitable for advanced readers in philosophy, literary theory, or psychology. Best after basic narrative theory and some hermeneutics.   |

### 1.7 Postclassical, Feminist, Queer, Postcolonial & Unnatural Narratology

| Type & Subdomain                                                   | Title, Edition, Year                                                                                     | Author / Editor / Translator                | Description & Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                             | Target Audience & Characteristics                                                                                                                    |
| ------------------------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| `[Research] Feminist narratology` `[Feminist]`                     | *Gendered Interventions: Narrative Discourse in the Victorian Novel*, 1989 `[Intermediate]` `[Research]` | **Robyn R. Warhol**                         | Warhol’s book is one of the founding works of feminist narratology, arguing that feminism and narratology need not be opposed. It shows how narratological precision can identify gendered features of discourse, address, affect, and textual strategy. Its limitation is that its corpus is Victorian fiction, so its model needs extension for non-Western, queer, postcolonial, and media narratives. Ohio State’s description stresses its reconciliation of feminist historical context with narratology’s precise method and vocabulary.  | Suitable after Bal, Booth, and basic feminist literary theory. Essential for readers who want formal analysis and gender analysis together.          |
| `[Anthology] Feminist narratology` `[Feminist]`                    | *Feminist Narratology and British Women Writers*, 1999 `[Intermediate]` `[Anthology]`                    | **Kathy Mezei**, ed.                        | This collection extends feminist narratology across women’s writing and narrative form. Its value is that it shows feminist narratology as a method rather than a single doctrine. Its limitation is that anthology quality varies and some chapters are more historical-literary than narratological. It is useful after Warhol for seeing the field’s applications.                                                                                                                                                                                              | Suitable for students in feminist literary studies. Requires basic narratology. Best for research on gender, voice, authorship, and women’s fiction. |
| `[Research] Unnatural narratology` `[Unnatural]` `[Postclassical]` | *Unnatural Narrative: Theory, History, and Practice*, 2015 `[Advanced]` `[Research]`                     | **Brian Richardson**                        | Richardson develops a theory of narratives that violate mimetic expectations: impossible temporalities, contradictory worlds, antimimetic narration, and strange narrative voices. Its value is that it prevents narratology from treating realist fiction as the hidden norm. Its limitation is that unnatural narratology can overprivilege exceptional texts and understate ordinary narrative cognition. It is essential for postmodern, experimental, and anti-mimetic fiction.                                                                               | Suitable after Genette, Fludernik, and Ryan. Best for advanced research on impossible storyworlds and experimental narrative.                        |
| `[Research] Postcolonial narratology` `[Postcolonial]`             | *Postcolonial Narratologies*, 2011 `[Advanced]` `[Anthology]`                                            | **Gerald Prince**, **John C. Hawley**, eds. | This collection brings narratology into contact with colonial history, global literature, race, empire, and cultural difference. It matters because classical narratology often universalized categories derived from European prose fiction. Its limitation is that the field remains methodologically uneven: some essays use narratological concepts more precisely than others. It is useful for testing whether narrative categories travel across histories and cultures.                                                                                    | Suitable after basic narratology and postcolonial theory. Best for graduate students working across form, empire, and global narrative.              |
| `[Research] Queer narrative theory` `[Queer]` `[Postclassical]`    | *Queer Times, Black Futures*, 2019 `[Advanced]` `[Bridge]`                                               | **Kara Keeling**                            | Keeling is not a conventional narratology textbook, but it is valuable for thinking about temporality, futurity, race, sexuality, media, and nonnormative forms of worldmaking. Its inclusion is justified when narrative theory is extended toward queer temporality and speculative social imagination. Its limitation is that readers seeking technical narratological vocabulary will need Bal, Genette, or Phelan first. It works as a bridge between queer theory, media theory, and narrative temporality.                                                  | Suitable for advanced interdisciplinary readers. Best used after classical narratology and queer theory. Not a first book in narrative theory.       |

### 1.8 Transmedial Narratology: Film, Television, Comics, Games & Digital Narrative

| Type & Subdomain                                                           | Title, Edition, Year                                                                                                           | Author / Editor / Translator                                   | Description & Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                            | Target Audience & Characteristics                                                                                                                                     |
| -------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| `[Research] Transmedial narratology` `[Transmedial]`                       | *Narrative across Media: The Languages of Storytelling*, 2004 `[Intermediate]` `[Anthology]`                                   | **Marie-Laure Ryan**, ed.                                      | This collection is central for understanding how narrative travels across prose, film, drama, painting, digital media, and other semiotic systems. Its value lies in refusing both extremes: narrative is not identical in every medium, but it is also not imprisoned within the novel. Its limitation is that edited volumes vary by chapter and some media have developed substantially since 2004. It remains an essential bridge text for transmedial narratology.                                           | Suitable after Bal or Chatman. Best for students moving from literary narratology into film, games, comics, and digital media.                                        |
| `[Research] Film narrative` `[Film]`                                       | *Narration in the Fiction Film*, 1985 `[Intermediate]` `[Evergreen]`                                                           | **David Bordwell**                                             | Bordwell offers a rigorous account of film narration, viewer activity, style, cues, comprehension, and cinematic norms. Its importance lies in showing that film narrative cannot simply borrow prose categories; camera, editing, sound, and spectatorship change the problem. Its limitation is that it is more film-theoretical than narratological in the narrow literary sense. It remains indispensable for film narratology.                                                                               | Suitable for readers with basic film studies background. Best paired with Chatman and Ryan. Essential for cinema, spectatorship, and audiovisual narration.           |
| `[Research] Comics narratology` `[Comics]`                                 | *The System of Comics*, French original 1999; English trans. 2007 `[Intermediate]` `[Research]`                                | **Thierry Groensteen**; trans. **Bart Beaty**, **Nick Nguyen** | Groensteen gives a formal theory of comics based on panels, page layout, arthrology, braiding, sequence, and spatial organization. It is important because comics narrative is not simply illustrated prose; spatial coexistence and sequential reading interact. Its limitation is theoretical abstraction, which can be difficult without examples in front of the reader. It is foundational for comics narratology.                                                                                           | Suitable after basic narratology and some comics studies. Best paired with McCloud for accessibility, though Groensteen is theoretically stronger.                    |
| `[Research] Game narrative` `[Game Studies]`                               | *Hamlet on the Holodeck: The Future of Narrative in Cyberspace*, updated ed., 2017; first ed. 1997 `[Introductory]` `[Bridge]` | **Janet H. Murray**                                            | Murray is a foundational voice in digital and interactive narrative, focusing on immersion, agency, transformation, and procedural environments. The book matters because it framed digital media as narrative environments before game studies fully institutionalized its own debates. Its limitation is that some early cyberculture optimism now needs historical correction, and later ludology/narratology debates complicate its claims. It is still a useful conceptual entry into interactive narrative. | Suitable for beginners in digital narrative and game studies. Best followed by Ryan and game-specific research.                                                       |
| `[Research] Transmedia storytelling` `[Transmedial]` `[Digital Narrative]` | *Convergence Culture: Where Old and New Media Collide*, 2006 `[Intermediate]` `[Bridge]`                                       | **Henry Jenkins**                                              | Jenkins is not a technical narratologist, but his account of transmedia storytelling is crucial for modern media narrative across franchises, fan cultures, platforms, and participatory media. Its strength is media ecology rather than formal taxonomy. Its limitation is that it can treat narrative expansion sociologically more than analytically. It should be read with Ryan for narratological precision.                                                                                               | Suitable for media studies students. Useful for seriality, fandom, worldbuilding, and platformed narrative. Not sufficient as a formal theory of narrative structure. |

### 1.9 Nonfiction, Life Writing, History, Law, Medicine & Social Narrative

| Type & Subdomain                                                     | Title, Edition, Year                                                                                          | Author / Editor / Translator                                         | Description & Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  | Target Audience & Characteristics                                                                                                                                        |
| -------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ |
| `[Foundational] Historical narrative` `[Historiography]`             | *Metahistory: The Historical Imagination in Nineteenth-Century Europe*, 1973 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]`       | **Hayden White**                                                     | White argues that historical writing is shaped by narrative emplotment, tropes, argument, and ideology. The book is crucial because it forced historians and literary theorists to confront the narrativity of historiography. Its limitation is that critics argue it risks aestheticizing or relativizing historical truth. It remains unavoidable for historical narrative and the boundary between fact and narrative form.                         | Suitable for advanced readers in history, literary theory, and philosophy of history. Best after basic narratology and historiography.                                   |
| `[Research] Time and narrative` `[Historiography]` `[Philosophical]` | *Time and Narrative*, French original 1983–1985; English trans. 1984–1988 `[Advanced]` `[Bridge]`             | **Paul Ricoeur**; trans. **Kathleen McLaughlin**, **David Pellauer** | Ricoeur connects narrative configuration, temporality, historiography, fiction, mimesis, and human action. It is one of the deepest philosophical accounts of why narrative matters for temporal understanding. Its limitation is density: it is too difficult as a first narratology text and often indirect in its relation to technical terms like focalization or voice. It is essential for philosophical narratology.                             | Suitable for advanced readers. Best after Genette, Aristotle’s *Poetics*, and some hermeneutics. Useful for time, history, fiction, and identity.                        |
| `[Research] Life writing` `[Autobiography]`                          | *Reading Autobiography: A Guide for Interpreting Life Narratives*, 2nd ed., 2010 `[Introductory]` `[Methods]` | **Sidonie Smith**, **Julia Watson**                                  | Smith and Watson provide a strong guide to autobiography, memoir, testimony, life writing, identity, memory, embodiment, and historical context. Its strength is that it treats life narrative as a genre and practice with specific conventions, not merely as “true fiction.” Its limitation is that it is more life-writing studies than formal narratology. It is highly useful for nonfiction narrative, testimony, and autobiographical analysis. | Suitable for beginners and intermediate readers. Strong for literary studies, history, gender studies, and cultural studies. Pair with Lejeune for autobiography theory. |
| `[Interdisciplinary] Narrative medicine` `[Medical Humanities]`      | *Narrative Medicine: Honoring the Stories of Illness*, 2006 `[Intermediate]` `[Bridge]`                       | **Rita Charon**                                                      | Charon develops narrative medicine as a clinical-humanistic practice of attending to illness stories, patient experience, and interpretation. It is valuable because it shows narrative theory entering a concrete professional field without becoming merely metaphorical. Its limitation is that it is not a technical narratology work and should be supplemented with formal narrative theory. It is central for medical humanities.                | Suitable for medical humanities, clinicians, and humanities researchers. Best after a basic narratology introduction if formal analysis is needed.                       |
| `[Interdisciplinary] Legal narrative` `[Legal Narrative]`            | *Narrative, Authority, and Law*, 1994 `[Advanced]` `[Bridge]`                                                 | **Robin West**                                                       | West examines how legal authority, interpretation, and subjectivity are shaped by narrative forms and normative imagination. Its value lies in connecting narrative to law’s construction of persons, injuries, rights, and responsibility. Its limitation is that it belongs more to jurisprudence and legal theory than to technical narratology. It is best used when studying narrative as institutional reasoning.                                 | Suitable for readers with legal theory or jurisprudence background. Useful for law-and-literature, testimony, legal storytelling, and narrative authority.               |

### 1.10 Methods, Handbooks, Anthologies & Long-Term Reference Works

| Type & Subdomain                            | Title, Edition, Year                                                                                     | Author / Editor / Translator                                                    | Description & Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                          | Target Audience & Characteristics                                                                                                                                         |
| ------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| `[Reference] Major handbook`                | *Handbook of Narratology*, 2nd ed., 2014 `[Reference]` `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]`                        | **Peter Hühn**, **Jan Christoph Meister**, **John Pier**, **Wolf Schmid**, eds. | This is one of the strongest long-term reference works in narratology, especially for concepts, research positions, historical development, and future directions. Its strength is systematic coverage of central terms rather than single-author doctrine. Its limitation is that it is not meant for linear first reading. The Living Handbook of Narratology notes that the online LHN is based on the 2009 handbook and its second, revised and expanded 2014 edition.           | Best for advanced students and researchers. Use chapter by chapter when a concept becomes important.                                                                      |
| `[Reference] Open-access handbook`          | *The Living Handbook of Narratology*, online `[Reference]` `[Evergreen]`                                 | **Peter Hühn** and collaborators, eds.                                          | LHN is the most useful open-access reference for narratological terminology. It provides entries on autobiography, character, cognitive narratology, focalization, metalepsis, narrator, plot, unreliable narration, and many other concepts. Its strength is accessibility and conceptual precision; its limitation is that handbook entries orient rather than replace full books. The official site describes it as based on the *Handbook of Narratology*.                                       | Essential desk reference for all levels beyond the absolute beginner. Particularly useful for checking terms before writing essays or research proposals.                 |
| `[Reference] Narrative theory encyclopedia` | *Routledge Encyclopedia of Narrative Theory*, 2005 `[Reference]` `[Evergreen]`                           | **David Herman**, **Manfred Jahn**, **Marie-Laure Ryan**, eds.                  | This encyclopedia is broader and more compact than the *Handbook of Narratology*, with short entries across theorists, terms, genres, methods, and interdisciplinary fields. It is excellent for quick orientation, but not enough for mastering a concept. Its best use is triangulation: consult it alongside LHN and then move to the major monographs. Routledge lists it as a 752-page reference work.                                                                                     | Suitable as a reference at every level. Especially useful when encountering unfamiliar subfields such as legal narrative, natural narratology, or possible worlds theory. |
| `[Anthology] Narrative theory reader`       | *Narrative Theory: Critical Concepts in Literary and Cultural Studies*, 2004 `[Reference]` `[Anthology]` | **Mieke Bal**, ed.                                                              | This multi-volume collection gathers major essays in narrative theory and related cultural analysis. Its value is archival and pedagogical: it gives access to important debates without requiring the reader to reconstruct the field from scattered journals. Its limitation is size and unevenness; anthologies are maps of debate, not unified theories. Use it for research sampling and historical depth.                                                                                                 | Suitable for graduate students and instructors. Best used selectively by topic. Not a first introduction.                                                                 |
| `[Reference] Companion overview`            | *The Cambridge Companion to Narrative*, 2007 `[Intermediate]` `[Reference]` `[Bridge]`                   | **David Herman**, ed.                                                           | This companion provides a compact overview of narrative study across concepts, media, and methods. It is less encyclopedic than Routledge and less technical than the *Handbook of Narratology*, which makes it useful between textbook and reference levels. Its limitation is that companion chapters are introductions, not full research monographs. Cambridge lists the print publication as 19 July 2007 and the online publication as 28 September 2007.  | Suitable after Abbott or Bal. Good for course design and for seeing how narrative study divides into problems such as character, time, media, and interpretation.         |

### Suggested Reading Paths

**General narratology path:** Abbott, *The Cambridge Introduction to Narrative* → Bal, *Narratology* → Fludernik, *An Introduction to Narratology* → Herman et al., *Narrative Theory: Core Concepts and Critical Debates* → LHN / *Routledge Encyclopedia of Narrative Theory*.

**Classical and structuralist narratology path:** Russian Formalist essays → Propp, *Morphology of the Folktale* → Todorov, *The Poetics of Prose* → Genette, *Narrative Discourse* → Chatman, *Story and Discourse*.

**Voice, focalization, and unreliable narration path:** Genette, *Narrative Discourse* → Bal, *Narratology* → Booth, *The Rhetoric of Fiction* → Prince, *Narratology* → Nünning on unreliable narration → Genette, *Metalepsis*.

**Rhetorical narratology and ethics path:** Booth, *The Rhetoric of Fiction* → Rabinowitz, *Before Reading* → Phelan, *Reading Narrative as Rhetoric* → Booth, *The Company We Keep* → Phelan, *Living to Tell about It*.

**Cognitive narratology path:** Fludernik, *An Introduction to Narratology* → Fludernik, *Towards a “Natural” Narratology* → Herman, *Story Logic* → Palmer, *Fictional Minds* → Cohn, *Transparent Minds*.

**Feminist, postcolonial, and unnatural narratology path:** Warhol, *Gendered Interventions* → Mezei, *Feminist Narratology and British Women Writers* → *Postcolonial Narratologies* → Richardson, *Unnatural Narrative* → Herman et al., *Narrative Theory: Core Concepts and Critical Debates*.

**Film, comics, games, and digital narrative path:** Chatman, *Story and Discourse* → Ryan, *Narrative across Media* → Bordwell, *Narration in the Fiction Film* → Groensteen, *The System of Comics* → Murray, *Hamlet on the Holodeck* → Jenkins, *Convergence Culture*.

**Nonfiction, life writing, and historiography path:** White, *Metahistory* → Ricoeur, *Time and Narrative* → Smith & Watson, *Reading Autobiography* → Charon, *Narrative Medicine* → West, *Narrative, Authority, and Law*.

**Research-reference path:** *The Cambridge Companion to Narrative* → *Routledge Encyclopedia of Narrative Theory* → *Handbook of Narratology* → *The Living Handbook of Narratology* → Bal’s edited *Narrative Theory: Critical Concepts in Literary and Cultural Studies*.
